   <h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Hemp</h1> 
   <div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content"> 
    <div id="siteSub">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div> 
    <div id="contentSub"></div> 
    <div id="jump-to-nav" class="mw-jump">
      Jump to: 
     <a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 
     <a href="#p-search">search</a> 
    </div> 
    <div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr">
     <div role="note" class="hatnote">
      This article is about industrial and foodstuff products that are derived from hemp cultivars of the 
      <i>Cannabis</i> plant. For the usage of 
      <i>Cannabis</i> as a drug, see 
      <a href="/wiki/Cannabis_(drug)" title="Cannabis (drug)">Cannabis (drug)</a>. For other uses, see 
      <a href="/wiki/Hemp_(disambiguation)" title="Hemp (disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig">Hemp (disambiguation)</a>.
     </div>

       <tr> 
        <td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"> 
         <div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"> 
          <ul> 
           <li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Cannabis_sidebar" title="Template:Cannabis sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li> 
           <li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Cannabis_sidebar" title="Template talk:Cannabis sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li> 
           <li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Cannabis_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li> 
          </ul> 
         </div> </td> 
       </tr> 
      </tbody>
     </table> 
     <p><b>Hemp</b> (from <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Cannabis_(etymology)" title="Cannabis (etymology)" class="mw-redirect">hænep</a></i>) is a commonly used term for high-growing industrial varieties of the <i><a href="/wiki/Cannabis" title="Cannabis">Cannabis</a></i> plant and its products, which include fiber, oil, and seed. Hemp is refined into products such as hemp seed foods, hemp oil, wax, resin, rope, cloth, pulp, paper, and fuel.</p> 
     <p>Hemp is not to be confused with the close relative marijuana, which is also a <i>Cannabis</i> plant, but is widely used as a recreational drug and medicine. These variants are typically low-growing and have higher content of <a href="/wiki/Tetrahydrocannabinol" title="Tetrahydrocannabinol">tetrahydrocannabinol</a> (THC), <a href="/wiki/Cannabidiol" title="Cannabidiol">cannabidiol</a> (CBD), and <a href="/wiki/Cannabinoid#Cannabis-derived_cannabinoids" title="Cannabinoid">other cannabinoids</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Legality_of_cannabis" title="Legality of cannabis">legality of <i>Cannabis</i></a> varies widely from country to country, and from state to state in the United States. In many countries regulatory limits for concentrations of psychoactive compounds, particularly THC, in hemp require the use of strains of the plant which are bred for low content.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup></p> 
     <p></p>

     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Hanf_zur_Milchviehf%C3%BCtterung.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Hanf_zur_Milchviehf%C3%BCtterung.jpg/220px-Hanf_zur_Milchviehf%C3%BCtterung.jpg" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Hanf_zur_Milchviehf%C3%BCtterung.jpg/330px-Hanf_zur_Milchviehf%C3%BCtterung.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Hanf_zur_Milchviehf%C3%BCtterung.jpg/440px-Hanf_zur_Milchviehf%C3%BCtterung.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1536" data-file-height="2048"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Hanf_zur_Milchviehf%C3%BCtterung.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> Hemp grown for milk animal 
        <a href="/wiki/Fodder" title="Fodder">fodder</a>.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Hemp is used for many varieties of products including the manufacture of <a href="/wiki/Cordage" title="Cordage" class="mw-redirect">cordage</a> of varying <a href="/wiki/Tension_(mechanics)" title="Tension (mechanics)" class="mw-redirect">tensile strength</a>, durable clothing and nutritional products. The <i><a href="/wiki/Bast_fiber" title="Bast fiber" class="mw-redirect">bast fibers</a></i> can be used in 100% hemp products, but are commonly blended with other organic fibers such as <a href="/wiki/Flax" title="Flax">flax</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cotton" title="Cotton">cotton</a> or <a href="/wiki/Silk" title="Silk">silk</a>, for apparel and furnishings, most commonly at a 55%/45% hemp/cotton blend. The inner two fibers of hemp are more woody and are more often used in non-woven items and other industrial applications, such as <a href="/wiki/Mulch" title="Mulch">mulch</a>, animal bedding and litter. The <a href="/wiki/Hemp_oil" title="Hemp oil">oil from the fruits</a> ("seeds") oxidizes (commonly, though inaccurately, called "drying") to become solid on exposure to air, similar to <a href="/wiki/Linseed" title="Linseed" class="mw-redirect">linseed</a> oil, and is sometimes used in the manufacture of oil-based paints, in creams as a moisturizing agent, for cooking, and in plastics. Hemp seeds have been used in bird feed mix as well.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> A survey in 2003 showed that more than 95% of hemp seed sold in the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">EU</a> was used in animal and bird feed.<sup id="cite_ref-michael_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-michael-3">[3]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>In modern times hemp is used for industrial purposes including <a href="/wiki/Paper" title="Paper">paper</a>, <a href="/wiki/Textiles" title="Textiles" class="mw-redirect">textiles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Clothing" title="Clothing">clothing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biodegradable_plastic" title="Biodegradable plastic">biodegradable plastics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Construction" title="Construction">construction</a> (as with <a href="/wiki/Hempcrete" title="Hempcrete">Hempcrete</a> and insulation), body products, health food and bio-fuel.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Food">Food</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hemp&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Food">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Hemp_bunch-dried_out_-seeds_close_up_PNr%C2%B00063.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Hemp_bunch-dried_out_-seeds_close_up_PNr%C2%B00063.jpg/220px-Hemp_bunch-dried_out_-seeds_close_up_PNr%C2%B00063.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Hemp_bunch-dried_out_-seeds_close_up_PNr%C2%B00063.jpg/330px-Hemp_bunch-dried_out_-seeds_close_up_PNr%C2%B00063.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Hemp_bunch-dried_out_-seeds_close_up_PNr%C2%B00063.jpg/440px-Hemp_bunch-dried_out_-seeds_close_up_PNr%C2%B00063.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="1944"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Hemp_bunch-dried_out_-seeds_close_up_PNr%C2%B00063.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> Hemp seeds.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Hemp seeds can be eaten raw, ground into a meal, sprouted or made into dried sprout powder, made into <a href="/wiki/Hemp_milk" title="Hemp milk">hemp milk</a> (akin to <a href="/wiki/Soy_milk" title="Soy milk">soy milk</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Hemp_juice" title="Hemp juice">hemp juice</a>, prepared as tea,<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> and used in baking. Roughly 30-35% of the mass of the seeds can be extracted as <a href="/wiki/Hempseed_oil" title="Hempseed oil" class="mw-redirect">hempseed oil</a>, which is rich in unsaturated fatty acids. The fresh leaves can also be consumed in salads. Products include cereals, frozen <a href="/wiki/Waffle" title="Waffle">waffles</a>, hemp milk ice cream, hemp tofu, and nut butters. A few companies produce <a href="/wiki/Value_added" title="Value added">value added</a> hemp seed items that include the seed oils, whole hemp <a href="/wiki/Grain" title="Grain">grain</a> (which is sterilized by law in the United States, where they import it from China and Canada), dehulled hemp seed (the whole seed without the mineral rich outer shell), hemp flour, hemp cake (a by-product of pressing the seed for oil) and hemp protein powder. Even though hemp and <a href="/wiki/Cannabis_(drug)" title="Cannabis (drug)">marijuana</a> are both made from the <i>Cannabis</i> plant, hemp seeds contain negligible levels of <a href="/wiki/Tetrahydrocannabinol" title="Tetrahydrocannabinol">tetrahydrocannabinol</a> (THC),<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> which is the <a href="/wiki/Psychoactive" title="Psychoactive" class="mw-redirect">psychoactive</a> substance in <a href="/wiki/Cannabis_(drug)" title="Cannabis (drug)">marijuana</a>.</p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Market_share">Market share</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hemp&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Market share">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
     <p>Within the UK, the <a href="/wiki/Department_for_Environment,_Food_and_Rural_Affairs" title="Department for Environment, Food and Rural Affairs">Department for Environment, Food and Rural Affairs</a> (Defra) has treated hemp as purely a non-food crop. Seed appears on the UK market as a legal food product, and cultivation licenses are available for this purpose. In North America, hemp seed food products are sold, typically in health food stores or through mail order. The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Agriculture" title="United States Department of Agriculture">United States Department of Agriculture</a> has concluded that "the market potential for hemp seed as a food ingredient is unknown. However, it probably will remain a small market, like those for sesame and poppy seeds."<sup id="cite_ref-ages_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ages-5">[5]</a></sup> In 2011, the U.S. imported $11.5 million worth of legal hemp products, up from $1.4 million in 2000, most of that driven by growth in hemp seed and hemp oil used in food products.<sup id="cite_ref-2014AgAct_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2014AgAct-6">[6]</a></sup></p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Nutrition">Nutrition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hemp&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Nutrition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
     <table class="wikitable" style="text-align:right; font-size:80%; float:right; margin-left:20px"> 
      <tbody>
       <tr> 
        <th colspan="2">Typical nutritional analysis<br> of hulled hemp seeds<sup id="cite_ref-nd_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nd-7">[7]</a></sup></th> 
       </tr> 
       <tr> 
        <th><a href="/wiki/Calorie" title="Calorie">Calories</a>/100 <a href="/wiki/Gram" title="Gram">g</a></th>

       </tr>

       </tr>

       </tr> 
      </tbody>
     </table> 
     <p>Hemp seeds are notable as a high-<a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">protein</a> food source, providing 73% of the Daily Value (DV) in a 100 g serving.<sup id="cite_ref-nd_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nd-7">[7]</a></sup> Hempseed <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino acid</a> profile is comparable to other sources of protein such as meat, milk, eggs and <a href="/wiki/Soy" title="Soy" class="mw-redirect">soy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-callaway_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-callaway-8">[8]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Protein_Digestibility_Corrected_Amino_Acid_Score" title="Protein Digestibility Corrected Amino Acid Score">Protein Digestibility Corrected Amino Acid Score</a> values (<a href="/wiki/PDCAAS" title="PDCAAS" class="mw-redirect">PDCAAS</a>), which measure the degree to which a food for humans is a "complete protein", were 0.49-0.53 for whole hemp seed, 0.46-0.51 for hemp seed meal, and 0.63-0.66 for dehulled hemp seed.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Hemp seeds are also a rich source of the <a href="/wiki/Dietary_minerals" title="Dietary minerals" class="mw-redirect">dietary minerals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">magnesium</a> (160% DV), <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">zinc</a> (77% DV) and <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a> (53% DV), and a good source of <a href="/wiki/Dietary_fiber" title="Dietary fiber">dietary fiber</a> (13% DV).<sup id="cite_ref-nd_7-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nd-7">[7]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Approximately 73% of the energy in hemp seeds is in the form of fats and <a href="/wiki/Essential_fatty_acids" title="Essential fatty acids" class="mw-redirect">essential fatty acids</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-nd_7-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nd-7">[7]</a></sup> mainly <a href="/wiki/Polyunsaturated_fatty_acids" title="Polyunsaturated fatty acids" class="mw-redirect">polyunsaturated fatty acids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Linoleic_acid" title="Linoleic acid">linoleic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oleic_acid" title="Oleic acid">oleic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alpha-linolenic_acid" title="Alpha-linolenic acid" class="mw-redirect">alpha-linolenic</a> acids.<sup id="cite_ref-callaway_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-callaway-8">[8]</a></sup></p> 
     <ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional"> 
      <li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px">

     <div style="clear:both;"></div> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Storage">Storage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hemp&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Storage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
     <p><a href="/wiki/Hemp_oil" title="Hemp oil">Hemp oil</a>, like any food oil rich in essential fatty acids, will spontaneously oxidize and turn rancid within a short period of time if not stored properly; its shelf life is extended when stored in a dark airtight container and refrigerated.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fiber">Fiber</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hemp&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Fiber">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Hanfstengel.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Hanfstengel.jpg/220px-Hanfstengel.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Hanfstengel.jpg/330px-Hanfstengel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Hanfstengel.jpg/440px-Hanfstengel.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1536"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Hanfstengel.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> Hemp stem showing fibers.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Hemp fiber has been used extensively throughout history, with production climaxing soon after being introduced to the New World. Items ranging from rope, to fabrics, to industrial materials were made from hemp fiber. Hemp was often used to make sail <a href="/wiki/Canvas" title="Canvas">canvas</a>, and the word <i>canvas</i> derives from <i>cannabis</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> Today, a modest hemp fabric industry exists, and hemp fibers can be used in clothing.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Pure hemp has a texture similar to <a href="/wiki/Linen" title="Linen">linen</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></p> 
     <ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional"> 
      <li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px">

       </div> </li> 
     </ul> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Building_material">Building material</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hemp&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Building material">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/Hempcrete" title="Hempcrete">hempcrete</a>
     </div> 
     <p>Concrete-like blocks made with hemp and lime have been used as an insulating material for construction. Such blocks are not strong enough to be used for structural elements; they must be supported by a brick, wood, or steel frame.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> However hemp fibres are extremely strong and durable, and have been shown to be usable as a replacement for wood for many jobs including creating very durable and breathable homes.</p> 
     <p>The first example of the use of hempcrete was in 1986 in France with the renovation of the Maison de la Turque in <a href="/wiki/Nogent-sur-Seine" title="Nogent-sur-Seine">Nogent-sur-Seine</a> by the innovator Charles Rasetti.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> In the UK hemp lime was first used in 2000 for the construction of two test dwellings in Haverhill.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> Designed by Modece Architects,<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> who pioneered hemp's use in UK construction, the hemp houses were monitored in comparison with other standard dwellings by BRE. Completed in 2009, The Renewable House is one of the most technologically advanced made from hemp-based materials.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> The first US home made of hemp-based materials was completed in August 2010 in Asheville, North Carolina.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>A panellized system of hemp-lime panels for use in building construction is currently under test in a European Union funded research collaboration led by the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Bath" title="University of Bath">University of Bath</a>. The panels are being designed to assure high quality construction, rapid on-site erection, optimal hygrothermal performance from day one and energy and resource efficient buildings. The 36-month-long work programme aims to refine product and manufacturing protocols, produce data for certification and marketing, warranty, insurance cover and availability of finance. It also includes the development of markets in Britain, France and Spain.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Hemp is used as an internal plaster and is a mixture of hemp hurd (shive) mixed with larger proportions of a lime based binder. Hemp plaster has insulative qualities.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p>

     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Plastic_and_composite_materials">Plastic and composite materials</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hemp&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Plastic and composite materials">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/Bioplastic" title="Bioplastic">Bioplastic</a>
     </div> 
     <p>A mixture of <a href="/wiki/Fiberglass" title="Fiberglass">fiberglass</a>, hemp fiber, <a href="/wiki/Kenaf" title="Kenaf">kenaf</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Flax" title="Flax">flax</a> has been used since 2002 to make composite panels for automobiles.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> The choice of which <a href="/wiki/Bast_fiber" title="Bast fiber" class="mw-redirect">bast fiber</a> to use is primarily based on cost and availability. Various car makers are beginning to use hemp in their cars, including <a href="/wiki/Audi" title="Audi">Audi</a>, <a href="/wiki/BMW" title="BMW">BMW</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ford" title="Ford" class="mw-redirect">Ford</a>, <a href="/wiki/General_Motors" title="General Motors">GM</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chrysler" title="Chrysler">Chrysler</a>, <a href="/wiki/Honda" title="Honda">Honda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iveco" title="Iveco">Iveco</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lotus_Cars" title="Lotus Cars">Lotus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mercedes-Benz" title="Mercedes-Benz">Mercedes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mitsubishi" title="Mitsubishi">Mitsubishi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Porsche" title="Porsche">Porsche</a>, Saturn, <a href="/wiki/Volkswagen" title="Volkswagen">Volkswagen</a><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> and Volvo. For example, the Lotus Eco Elise<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> and the Mercedes C-Class both contain hemp (up to 20&nbsp;kg in each car in the case of the latter).<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p> 
     <ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional"> 
      <li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px">

     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Paper">Paper</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hemp&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Paper">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="History_and_development">History and development</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hemp&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: History and development">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Chinese_hemp_paper_western_han.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Chinese_hemp_paper_western_han.jpg/220px-Chinese_hemp_paper_western_han.jpg" width="220" height="214" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Chinese_hemp_paper_western_han.jpg/330px-Chinese_hemp_paper_western_han.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Chinese_hemp_paper_western_han.jpg/440px-Chinese_hemp_paper_western_han.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1160" data-file-height="1128"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Chinese_hemp_paper_western_han.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> 
        <a href="/wiki/Wrapping_paper" title="Wrapping paper" class="mw-redirect">Wrapping paper</a> with hemp fiber excavated from the 
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Han_Tomb&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Han Tomb (page does not exist)">Han Tomb</a> of 
        <a href="/wiki/Three_Sovereigns_and_Five_Emperors" title="Three Sovereigns and Five Emperors">Wu Di</a> (140-87 BC) at 
        <a href="/wiki/Baqiao" title="Baqiao" class="mw-redirect">Baqiao</a>, Xi'An.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>The first identified coarse <a href="/wiki/Paper" title="Paper">paper</a>, made from hemp, dates to the early <a href="/wiki/Western_Han_Dynasty" title="Western Han Dynasty" class="mw-redirect">Western Han Dynasty</a>, two hundred years before the nominal invention of <a href="/wiki/Papermaking" title="Papermaking">papermaking</a> by <a href="/wiki/Cai_Lun" title="Cai Lun">Cai Lun</a>, who improved and standardized paper production using a range of inexpensive materials, including hemp ends, approximately 2000 years ago.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> Recycled hemp clothing, rags and fishing nets were used as inputs for paper production.</p> 
     <p>The <a href="/wiki/Saint_Petersburg,_Russia" title="Saint Petersburg, Russia" class="mw-redirect">Saint Petersburg, Russia</a>, paper mill of Goznak opened in 1818. It used hemp as its main input material. Paper from the mill was used in the printing of "<a href="/wiki/Bank_note" title="Bank note" class="mw-redirect">bank notes</a>, stamped paper, credit bills, postal stamps, bonds, stocks, and other <a href="/wiki/Watermark" title="Watermark">watermarked</a> paper."<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>In 1916, U.S. Department of Agriculture chief scientists <a href="/wiki/Lyster_Hoxie_Dewey" title="Lyster Hoxie Dewey">Lyster Hoxie Dewey</a> and Jason L. Merrill created paper made from hemp pulp and concluded that paper from hemp hurds was "favorable in comparison with those used with pulp wood."<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> The chemical composition of hemp hurds is similar to that of wood,<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> making hemp a good choice as a raw material for manufacturing paper. Modern research has not confirmed the positive finding about hemp hurds from 1916. A later book about hemp and other fibers by the same L.H. Dewey(1943) have no words about hemp as a raw material for production of paper.<sup id="cite_ref-Dew_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dew-31">[31]</a></sup> Dried hemp has about 57% <a href="/wiki/Cellulose" title="Cellulose">cellulose</a> (the principal ingredient in paper), compared to about 40-50% in wood.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> Hemp also has the advantage of a lower lignin content: hemp contains only 5-24% lignin<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> against the 20-35% found in wood.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> This lignin must be removed chemically and wood requires more use of chemicals in the process.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> The actual production of hemp fiber in the U.S continued to decline until 1933 to around 500 tons/year. Between 1934-35, the cultivation of hemp began to increase but still at a very low level and with no significant increase of paper from hemp.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-News_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-News-39">[39]</a></sup></p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Contemporary">Contemporary</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hemp&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Contemporary">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
     <p>Currently there is a small niche market for hemp pulp, for example as cigarette paper.<sup id="cite_ref-Kraus_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kraus-40">[40]</a></sup> Hemp fiber is mixed with fiber from other sources than hemp. In 1994 there was no significant production of 100% true hemp paper.<sup id="cite_ref-hempline_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hempline-41">[41]</a></sup> World hemp pulp production was believed to be around 120,000 tons per year in 1991 which was about 0.05% of the world's annual pulp production volume.<sup id="cite_ref-Gertjan_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gertjan-42">[42]</a></sup> The total world production of hemp fiber had in 2003 declined to about 60,000 from 80,000 tons.<sup id="cite_ref-Kraus_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kraus-40">[40]</a></sup> This can be compared to a typical pulp mill for wood fiber, which is never smaller than 250,000 tons per annum.<sup id="cite_ref-hempline_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hempline-41">[41]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> The cost of hemp pulp is approximately six times that of wood pulp,<sup id="cite_ref-Gertjan_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gertjan-42">[42]</a></sup> mostly because of the small size and outdated equipment of the few hemp processing plants in the Western world, and because hemp is harvested once a year (during August)<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> and needs to be stored to feed the mill the whole year through. This storage requires a lot of (mostly manual) handling of the bulky stalk bundles. Another issue is that the entire hemp plant cannot be economically prepared for paper production. While the wood products industry uses nearly 100% of the fiber from harvested trees, only about 25% of the dried hemp stem — the bark, called bast — contains the long, strong fibers desirable for paper production.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> All this accounts for a high raw material cost. Hemp pulp is bleached with <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_peroxide" title="Hydrogen peroxide">hydrogen peroxide</a>, a process today also commonly used for wood pulp.</p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Market_share_2">Market share</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hemp&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Market share">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
     <p>Around the year 2000, the production quantity of flax and hemp pulp total 25000-30000 tons per year, having been produced from approximately 37000-45000 tonnes fibers. Up to 80% of the produced pulp is used for specialty papers (including 95% of <a href="/wiki/Cigarette_paper" title="Cigarette paper" class="mw-redirect">cigarette paper</a>). Only about 20% hemp fiber input goes into the standard pulp area and are here mostly in lower quality (untreated <a href="/wiki/Oakum" title="Oakum">oakum</a> high <a href="/wiki/Shive" title="Shive">shive</a> content added) wood pulps. With hemp pulp alone, the proportion of specialty papers probably at about 99%. The market is considered saturated with little or no growth in this area.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Jewelry">Jewelry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hemp&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Jewelry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/Hemp_jewelry" title="Hemp jewelry">Hemp jewelry</a>
     </div> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Hemp_and_Bead_Jewelry,_Oakland_Chinatown_Street_Fair.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Hemp_and_Bead_Jewelry%2C_Oakland_Chinatown_Street_Fair.jpg/220px-Hemp_and_Bead_Jewelry%2C_Oakland_Chinatown_Street_Fair.jpg" width="220" height="189" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Hemp_and_Bead_Jewelry%2C_Oakland_Chinatown_Street_Fair.jpg/330px-Hemp_and_Bead_Jewelry%2C_Oakland_Chinatown_Street_Fair.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Hemp_and_Bead_Jewelry%2C_Oakland_Chinatown_Street_Fair.jpg/440px-Hemp_and_Bead_Jewelry%2C_Oakland_Chinatown_Street_Fair.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2280" data-file-height="1962"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Hemp_and_Bead_Jewelry,_Oakland_Chinatown_Street_Fair.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> Hemp and bead Jewelry.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p><a href="/wiki/Hemp_jewelry" title="Hemp jewelry">Hemp jewelry</a> is the product of knotting hemp twine through the practice of <a href="/wiki/Macram%C3%A9" title="Macramé">macramé</a>. Hemp jewellery includes bracelets, necklaces, anklets, rings, watches and other adornments. Some jewellery features beads made from <a href="/wiki/Glass" title="Glass">glass</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stone" title="Stone" class="mw-redirect">stone</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wood" title="Wood">wood</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bone" title="Bone">bones</a>. The hemp twine varies in thickness and comes in a variety of colors. There are many different <a href="/wiki/Stitch_(textile_arts)" title="Stitch (textile arts)">stitches</a> used to create hemp jewellery, however, the half knot and full knot stitches are most common.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cordage">Cordage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hemp&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Cordage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Cordage_en_chanvre.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Cordage_en_chanvre.jpg/220px-Cordage_en_chanvre.jpg" width="220" height="172" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Cordage_en_chanvre.jpg/330px-Cordage_en_chanvre.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Cordage_en_chanvre.jpg/440px-Cordage_en_chanvre.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2980" data-file-height="2324"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Cordage_en_chanvre.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> Hemp rope.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Hemp rope was used in the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Sail" title="Age of Sail">age of sailing ships</a>, though the rope had to be protected by <a href="/wiki/Tarring_(rope)" title="Tarring (rope)">tarring</a>, since hemp rope has a propensity for breaking from <a href="/wiki/Decomposition" title="Decomposition">rot</a>, as the capillary effect of the rope-woven fibers tended to hold liquid at the interior, while seeming dry from the outside.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> Tarring was a labor-intensive process, and earned sailors the nickname "<a href="/wiki/Jack_Tar" title="Jack Tar">Jack Tar</a>". Hemp rope was phased out when Manila, which does not require tarring, became widely available. Manila is sometimes referred to as <a href="/wiki/Manila_hemp" title="Manila hemp">Manila hemp</a>, but is not related to hemp; it is <a href="/wiki/Abac%C3%A1" title="Abacá">abacá</a>, a species of <a href="/wiki/Banana" title="Banana">banana</a>.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Animal_bedding">Animal bedding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hemp&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Animal bedding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Hemp_Straw.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Hemp_Straw.JPG/220px-Hemp_Straw.JPG" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Hemp_Straw.JPG/330px-Hemp_Straw.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Hemp_Straw.JPG/440px-Hemp_Straw.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2736" data-file-height="3648"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Hemp_Straw.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> Hemp straw animal bedding.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Hemp shives are the core of the stem, hemp hurds are broken parts of the core. In the EU, they are used for animal bedding (horses, for instance), or for horticultural mulch.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Industrial hemp is much more profitable if both fibers and shives (or even seeds) can be used.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Water_and_soil_purification">Water and soil purification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hemp&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Water and soil purification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <p>Hemp can be used as a "mop crop" to clear impurities out of wastewater, such as sewage effluent, excessive phosphorus from chicken litter, or other unwanted substances or chemicals. Additionally Hemp is being used to clean contaminants at the <a href="/wiki/Chernobyl_disaster" title="Chernobyl disaster">Chernobyl nuclear disaster</a> site. By way of a process which is known as <a href="/wiki/Phytoremediation" title="Phytoremediation">phytoremediation</a> - the process of clearing radioisotopes as well as a variety of other toxins from the soil, water, and air.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Weed_control">Weed control</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hemp&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Weed control">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/Weed_control#Organic_methods" title="Weed control">Weed control § Organic methods</a>
     </div> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Hanflabyrinth_Berlin_2009_-_40.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Hanflabyrinth_Berlin_2009_-_40.jpg/220px-Hanflabyrinth_Berlin_2009_-_40.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Hanflabyrinth_Berlin_2009_-_40.jpg/330px-Hanflabyrinth_Berlin_2009_-_40.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Hanflabyrinth_Berlin_2009_-_40.jpg/440px-Hanflabyrinth_Berlin_2009_-_40.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="426"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Hanflabyrinth_Berlin_2009_-_40.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> The dense growth of hemp helps kill weeds, even 
        <a href="/wiki/Thistle" title="Thistle">thistle</a>.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Hemp, because of its height, dense foliage and its high planting density as a crop, is a very effective and long used method of killing tough weeds in farming by minimizing the pool of weed seeds of the soil.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> Using hemp this way can help farmers avoid the use of herbicides, to help gain <a href="/wiki/Organic_certification" title="Organic certification">organic certification</a> and to gain the benefits of <a href="/wiki/Crop_rotation" title="Crop rotation">crop rotation</a>. Due to its rapid, dense growth characteristics, in some jurisdictions hemp is considered a prohibited noxious weed, much like <a href="/wiki/Cytisus_scoparius" title="Cytisus scoparius">Scotch Broom</a>. It has been used extensively to kill weeds in agriculture.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biofuels">Biofuels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hemp&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Biofuels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <p><a href="/wiki/Biodiesel" title="Biodiesel">Biodiesel</a> can be made from the oils in hemp seeds and stalks and <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_fuel" title="Alcohol fuel">alcohol fuel</a> (ethanol or, less commonly, methanol) from the fermentation of the whole plant. Biodiesel produced from hemp is sometimes known as "hempoline".<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></p> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Biodiesel.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Biodiesel.JPG/220px-Biodiesel.JPG" width="220" height="242" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Biodiesel.JPG/330px-Biodiesel.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Biodiesel.JPG/440px-Biodiesel.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2144" data-file-height="2356"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Biodiesel.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> Biodiesel sample.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Filtered hemp oil can be used directly to power <a href="/wiki/Diesel_engine" title="Diesel engine">diesel engines</a>. In 1892, <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Diesel" title="Rudolf Diesel">Rudolf Diesel</a> invented the diesel engine, which he intended to power "by a variety of fuels, especially vegetable and seed oils, which earlier were used for <a href="/wiki/Oil_lamp" title="Oil lamp">oil lamps</a>, i.e. the <a href="/wiki/Argand_lamp" title="Argand lamp">Argand lamp</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Production of vehicle fuel from hemp is very small. Commercial <a href="/wiki/Biodiesel" title="Biodiesel">biodiesel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Biogas" title="Biogas">biogas</a> is typically produced from cereals, coconuts, palmseeds and cheaper raw materials like garbage, wastewater, dead plant and animal material, animal <a href="/wiki/Feces" title="Feces">feces</a> and kitchen waste.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultivation">Cultivation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hemp&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Cultivation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Hempharvesting2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Hempharvesting2.jpg/220px-Hempharvesting2.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Hempharvesting2.jpg/330px-Hempharvesting2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Hempharvesting2.jpg/440px-Hempharvesting2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="550" data-file-height="413"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Hempharvesting2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> Hemp being harvested
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Hemp is usually planted between March and May in the northern hemisphere, between September and November in the southern hemisphere.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> It matures in about three to four months.</p> 
     <p>Millennia of <a href="/wiki/Selective_breeding" title="Selective breeding">selective breeding</a> have resulted in <a href="/wiki/Cultivar" title="Cultivar">varieties</a> that look quite different. Also, breeding since circa 1930 has focused quite specifically on producing strains which would perform very poorly as sources of drug material. Hemp grown for fiber is planted closely, resulting in tall, slender plants with long fibers. "Until the early 1900s industrial hemp was a valuable crop used all over the world for its strong fibers and oil seeds. Today, however, the common perception of the industrial hemp plant is generally negative and associated with the drug <a href="/wiki/Marijuana" title="Marijuana" class="mw-redirect">marijuana</a>. This perception is the legacy of a century of powerful influences construing hemp as a dangerous drug, even though it is not a drug and it has the potential to be a profitable alternative crop. In the United States, the public's perception of hemp as marijuana has blocked hemp from becoming a useful crop and product,"<sup id="cite_ref-luginbuhl.industrial.hemp_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-luginbuhl.industrial.hemp-57">[57]</a></sup> in spite of its vital importance prior to World War II.<sup id="cite_ref-Robert_Deitch_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Robert_Deitch-58">[58]</a></sup> Ideally, according to Britain's <a href="/wiki/Department_for_Environment,_Food_and_Rural_Affairs" title="Department for Environment, Food and Rural Affairs">Department for Environment, Food and Rural Affairs</a>, the herb should be desiccated and harvested towards the end of flowering. This early cropping reduces the seed yield but improves the fiber yield and quality.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> In these strains of industrial hemp the <a href="/wiki/Tetrahydrocannabinol" title="Tetrahydrocannabinol">tetrahydrocannabinol</a> (THC) content would have been very low.<sup id="cite_ref-luginbuhl.industrial.hemp_57-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-luginbuhl.industrial.hemp-57">[57]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>The seeds are sown from mid-April to mid-May with grain drills to 4–6&nbsp;cm sowing depth. Hemp needs less fertilizer than corn does. A total of 60–150&nbsp;kg of nitrogen, 40–140&nbsp;kg phosphorus (P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>) and 75–200&nbsp;kg of potassium [5] per acre for hemp fiber made before sowing and again later, maybe three to four weeks. When practiced, especially in France double use of fiber and seed fertilization with nitrogen doses up to 100&nbsp;kg / <a href="/wiki/Hectare" title="Hectare">ha</a> rather low. Organic fertilizers such as manure can utilize industrial hemp well. Neither weeds nor crop protection measures are necessary.<sup id="cite_ref-luginbuhl.industrial.hemp_57-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-luginbuhl.industrial.hemp-57">[57]</a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultivars">Cultivars</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hemp&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Cultivars">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Cannabis_Sativa_Querschnitt.JPG" class="image"><img alt="longitudinal section photo" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Cannabis_Sativa_Querschnitt.JPG/220px-Cannabis_Sativa_Querschnitt.JPG" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Cannabis_Sativa_Querschnitt.JPG/330px-Cannabis_Sativa_Querschnitt.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Cannabis_Sativa_Querschnitt.JPG/440px-Cannabis_Sativa_Querschnitt.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2736" data-file-height="3648"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Cannabis_Sativa_Querschnitt.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> 
        <i><a href="/wiki/Cannabis_sativa" title="Cannabis sativa">Cannabis sativa</a></i> stem
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:USO-xx_and_Zolotoniski-xx_hemp_strains_010.jpg" class="image"><img alt="low-angle photo-shot" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/USO-xx_and_Zolotoniski-xx_hemp_strains_010.jpg/220px-USO-xx_and_Zolotoniski-xx_hemp_strains_010.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/USO-xx_and_Zolotoniski-xx_hemp_strains_010.jpg/330px-USO-xx_and_Zolotoniski-xx_hemp_strains_010.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/USO-xx_and_Zolotoniski-xx_hemp_strains_010.jpg/440px-USO-xx_and_Zolotoniski-xx_hemp_strains_010.jpg 2x" data-file-width="700" data-file-height="467"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:USO-xx_and_Zolotoniski-xx_hemp_strains_010.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> Hemp strains USO-xx and Zolotoniski-xx
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>A total of 26 varieties of hemp with low levels of tetrahydrocannabinol (THC) are certified by the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> (EU).<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> They have, unlike other types, a very high fiber content of 30-40%. In contrast to cannabis for medical use, varieties grown for fiber and seed have less than 0.2% THC and they are unsuitable for producing hashish and marijuana.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> The most important cannabinoid in industrial hemp is <a href="/wiki/Cannabidiol" title="Cannabidiol">cannabidiol</a> (CBD) with a proportion of 1 to 5%.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Cannab2_new.png" class="image"><img alt="black and white drawing: C. sativa tall, C. indica middle, C. ruderalis small" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Cannab2_new.png/220px-Cannab2_new.png" width="220" height="309" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b3/Cannab2_new.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b3/Cannab2_new.png 2x" data-file-width="285" data-file-height="400"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Cannab2_new.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> The variety of appearances for cannabis. Only 
        <i>C. sativa</i> (left) is suited for industrial hemp, but it also has medicinal varieties.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p><i>Cannabis sativa</i> L. subsp. <i>sativa</i> var. <i>sativa</i> is the variety grown for industrial use, while <i>C. sativa</i> subsp. <i>indica</i> generally has poor fiber quality and is primarily used for recreational and medicinal purposes. The major differences between the two types of plants are the appearance, and the amount of Δ<sup>9</sup>-<a href="/wiki/Tetrahydrocannabinol" title="Tetrahydrocannabinol">tetrahydrocannabinol</a> (THC) secreted in a resinous mixture by <a href="/wiki/Epidermis_(botany)" title="Epidermis (botany)">epidermal</a> hairs called glandular <a href="/wiki/Trichomes" title="Trichomes" class="mw-redirect">trichomes</a>, although they can also be distinguished genetically.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> Oilseed and fiber varieties of <i>Cannabis</i> approved for industrial hemp production produce only minute amounts of this psychoactive drug, not enough for any physical or psychological effects. Typically, hemp contains below 0.3% THC, while cultivars of <i>Cannabis</i> grown for recreational use can contain anywhere from 2% to over 20%.<sup id="cite_ref-myths_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-myths-63">[63]</a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Harvesting">Harvesting</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hemp&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Harvesting">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Recoltechv.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Recoltechv.jpg/220px-Recoltechv.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Recoltechv.jpg/330px-Recoltechv.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Recoltechv.jpg/440px-Recoltechv.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2448" data-file-height="1632"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Recoltechv.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> Industrial hempseed harvesting machine in 
        <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Smallholder plots are usually harvested by hand. The plants are cut at 2 to 3&nbsp;cm above the soil and left on the ground to dry. Mechanical harvesting is now common, using specially adapted cutter-binders or simpler cutters.</p> 
     <p>The cut hemp is laid in swathes to dry for up to four days. This was traditionally followed by <i><a href="/wiki/Retting" title="Retting">retting</a></i>, either water retting (the bundled hemp floats in water) or dew retting (the hemp remains on the ground and is affected by the moisture in dew, and by molds and <a href="/wiki/Bacterial" title="Bacterial" class="mw-redirect">bacterial</a> action). Modern processes use steam and machinery to separate the fiber, a process known as <a href="/wiki/Pulp_(paper)#Thermomechanical_pulp" title="Pulp (paper)">thermomechanical pulping</a>.</p> 
     <ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional"> 
      <li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"> 
       <div style="width: 155px"> 
        <div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;"> 
         <div style="margin:15px auto;">

     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Location_and_crop_rotation">Location and crop rotation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hemp&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Location and crop rotation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:La_Roche_Jagu_chanvre_2.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/La_Roche_Jagu_chanvre_2.JPG/220px-La_Roche_Jagu_chanvre_2.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/La_Roche_Jagu_chanvre_2.JPG/330px-La_Roche_Jagu_chanvre_2.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/La_Roche_Jagu_chanvre_2.JPG/440px-La_Roche_Jagu_chanvre_2.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3264" data-file-height="2448"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:La_Roche_Jagu_chanvre_2.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> Hemp maze in France
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>For profitable hemp farming, particularly deep, <a href="/wiki/Humus" title="Humus">humus</a>-rich, nutrient-rich soil with controlled water flow is preferable. Water logged acidic, compressed or extremely light (sandy) soils primarily affect the early development of plants.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Steep and high altitudes of more than 400 m above sea level are best avoided. Hemp is relatively insensitive to cold temperatures and can withstand frost down to -5 degrees C.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Seeds can germinate down to 1-3 degrees.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Hemp needs a lot of heat, so earlier varieties come to maturation. The water requirement is 300-500 l / kg dry matter.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Up to 3 feet growing roots into the soil can also use water supplies from deeper soil layers. Worth noting is that the water requirement of hemp is at least 14 times lower than that of cotton which takes between 7 000-29 000 l/kg, according to WWF.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p> 
     <p>Hemp benefits crops grown after it. For this reason, it is generally grown before winter cereals. Advantageous changes are high weed suppression, soil loosening by the large hemp root system and the positive effect on soil <a href="/wiki/Tilth" title="Tilth">tilth</a>. Since hemp is very self-compatible, it can also be grown several years in a row in the same fields (monoculture).</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Diseases">Diseases</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hemp&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Diseases">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/List_of_hemp_diseases" title="List of hemp diseases">List of hemp diseases</a>
     </div> 
     <p>Hemp plants can be vulnerable to various <a href="/wiki/Pathogen" title="Pathogen">pathogens</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fungi" title="Fungi" class="mw-redirect">fungi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nematodes" title="Nematodes" class="mw-redirect">nematodes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Viruses" title="Viruses" class="mw-redirect">viruses</a> and other miscellaneous pathogens. Such diseases often lead to reduced fiber quality, stunted growth, and death of the plant. These diseases rarely affect the yield of a hemp field, so hemp production is not traditionally dependent on the use of pesticides.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_impact">Environmental impact</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hemp&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Environmental impact">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <p>Hemp is considered by a 1998 study in <i><a href="/wiki/Environmental_Economics" title="Environmental Economics" class="mw-redirect">Environmental Economics</a></i> to be <a href="/wiki/Environmentally_friendly" title="Environmentally friendly">environmentally friendly</a> due to a decrease of <a href="/wiki/Land_use" title="Land use">land use</a> and other environmental impacts, indicating a possible decrease of <a href="/wiki/Ecological_footprint" title="Ecological footprint">ecological footprint</a> in a US context compared to typical benchmarks.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> A 2010 study, however, that compared the production of <a href="/wiki/Paper" title="Paper">paper</a> specifically from hemp and <a href="/wiki/Eucalyptus" title="Eucalyptus">eucalyptus</a> concluded that "industrial hemp presents higher environmental impacts than eucalyptus paper"; however, the article also highlights that "there is scope for improving industrial hemp paper production".<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> Hemp is also claimed to require few <a href="/wiki/Pesticide" title="Pesticide">pesticides</a> and no herbicides, and it has been called a <a href="/wiki/Carbon_negative" title="Carbon negative" class="mw-redirect">carbon negative</a> raw material.<sup id="cite_ref-thehia_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thehia-66">[66]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-thehia2_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thehia2-67">[67]</a></sup> Results indicate that high yield of hemp may require high total nutrient levels (field plus fertilizer nutrients) similar to a high yielding wheat crop.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Producers">Producers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hemp&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Producers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
